     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Persian_literature" title="Persian literature">Persian literature</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Ferdowsi_tomb4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ferdowsi_tomb4.jpg/220px-Ferdowsi_tomb4.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ferdowsi_tomb4.jpg/330px-Ferdowsi_tomb4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ferdowsi_tomb4.jpg/440px-Ferdowsi_tomb4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Ferdowsi_tomb4.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Mausoleum of 
        <a href="/wiki/Ferdowsi" title="Ferdowsi">Ferdowsi</a> in 
        <a href="/wiki/Tus,_Iran" title="Tus, Iran">Tus</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Iranian literature is one of the world's oldest literatures, dating back to the poetry of <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">Avesta</a> and Zoroastrian literature.</p> 
     <p>Poetry is used in many Iranian classical works, whether in literature, science, or metaphysics. <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian language</a> has been dubbed as a worthy language to serve as a conduit for poetry, and is considered as one of the four main bodies of world literature.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">[323]</a></sup> Dialects of Persian are sporadically spoken throughout regions from China to Syria and Russia, though mainly in the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Plateau" title="Iranian Plateau">Iranian Plateau</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">[324]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">[325]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Iran has a number of famous poets; most notably <a href="/wiki/Rumi" title="Rumi">Rumi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferdowsi" title="Ferdowsi">Ferdowsi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hafez" title="Hafez">Hafez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saadi_Shirazi" title="Saadi Shirazi">Saadi Shirazi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khayy%C3%A1m" title="Khayyám" class="mw-redirect">Khayyám Ney-Shapuri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nezami_Ganjavi" title="Nezami Ganjavi" class="mw-redirect">Nezami Ganjavi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">[326]</a></sup> Historically, Iranian literature has inspired writers including <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Paul_Kane_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paul_Kane-88">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shafiq_Shamel_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shafiq_Shamel-89">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Adineh_Khojasteh_Pour_and_Behnam_Mirza_Baba_Zadeh_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adineh_Khojasteh_Pour_and_Behnam_Mirza_Baba_Zadeh-90">[89]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles: 
      <a href="/wiki/Ancient_philosophy#Ancient_Iranian_philosophy" title="Ancient philosophy">Ancient philosophy § Ancient Iranian philosophy</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Iranian_philosophy" title="Iranian philosophy">Iranian philosophy</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Farvahar001.JPG" class="image"><img alt="The Farohar Symbol from Persepolis" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Farvahar001.JPG/220px-Farvahar001.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Farvahar001.JPG/330px-Farvahar001.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Farvahar001.JPG/440px-Farvahar001.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1300" data-file-height="977"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Farvahar001.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Depiction of a 
        <a href="/wiki/Farvahar" title="Farvahar" class="mw-redirect">Fravarti</a> in 
        <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Iranian philosophy originates to <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranians" title="Indo-Iranians">Indo-Iranian</a> roots, with <a href="/wiki/Zarathustra" title="Zarathustra" class="mw-redirect">Zarathustra</a>'s teachings having major influences.</p> 
     <p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Dictionary_of_Philosophy" title="Oxford Dictionary of Philosophy" class="mw-redirect">Oxford Dictionary of Philosophy</a>, the chronology of the subject and science of philosophy starts with the Indo-Iranians, dating this event to 1500 BC. The Oxford dictionary also states, "<a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zarathushtra's philosophy</a> entered to influence Western tradition through <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, and therefore on <a href="/wiki/Middle_Platonism" title="Middle Platonism">Middle Platonism</a>."</p> 
     <p>While there are ancient relations between the Indian <i><a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a></i> and the Iranian <i><a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">Avesta</a></i>, the two main families of the Indo-Iranian philosophical traditions were characterized by fundamental differences, especially in their implications for the human being's position in society and their view of man's role in the universe.</p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_cylinder" title="Cyrus cylinder" class="mw-redirect">Cyrus cylinder</a>, which is known as "the first charter of <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>," is often seen as a reflection of the questions and thoughts expressed by <a href="/wiki/Zarathustra" title="Zarathustra" class="mw-redirect">Zarathustra</a>, and developed in Zoroastrian schools of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Era</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">[327]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">[328]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The earliest tenets of Zoroastrian schools are part of the extant scriptures of the Zoroastrian religion in <a href="/wiki/Avestan_language" title="Avestan language" class="mw-redirect">Avestan language</a>. Among them are treatises such as the <a href="/wiki/Shikand-gumanic_Vichar" title="Shikand-gumanic Vichar">Shikand-gumanic Vichar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denkard" title="Denkard">Denkard</a>, Zātspram, as well as older passages of Avesta, and the <a href="/wiki/Gathas" title="Gathas">Gathas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">[329]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mythology">Mythology</span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles: 
      <a href="/wiki/Iranian_mythology" title="Iranian mythology">Iranian mythology</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Iranian_folklore" title="Iranian folklore">Iranian folklore</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Arash_statue_in_Sa%27d%C4%81b%C4%81d_palace.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Arash_statue_in_Sa%27d%C4%81b%C4%81d_palace.jpg/220px-Arash_statue_in_Sa%27d%C4%81b%C4%81d_palace.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Arash_statue_in_Sa%27d%C4%81b%C4%81d_palace.jpg/330px-Arash_statue_in_Sa%27d%C4%81b%C4%81d_palace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Arash_statue_in_Sa%27d%C4%81b%C4%81d_palace.jpg/440px-Arash_statue_in_Sa%27d%C4%81b%C4%81d_palace.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1282" data-file-height="854"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Arash_statue_in_Sa%27d%C4%81b%C4%81d_palace.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Statue of 
        <a href="/wiki/Arash_the_Archer" title="Arash the Archer" class="mw-redirect">Arash the Archer</a> at the 
        <a href="/wiki/Sadabad_Palace" title="Sadabad Palace" class="mw-redirect">Sadabad Complex</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Iranian <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythology</a> consists of ancient <a href="/wiki/Iranian_folklore" title="Iranian folklore">Iranian folklore</a> and stories, all involving extraordinary beings. They reflect attitudes towards the confrontation of <a href="/wiki/Good_and_evil" title="Good and evil">good and evil</a>, actions of the gods, and the exploits of heroes and fabulous creatures.</p> 
     <p>Myths play a crucial part in the culture of Iran, and understanding of them is increased when they are considered within the context of actual events in the history of Iran. The geography of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Greater Iran</a>, a vast area covering the present-day Iran, <a href="/wiki/The_Caucasus" title="The Caucasus" class="mw-redirect">the Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, with its high mountain ranges, plays the main role in much of the Iranian mythology.</p> 
     <p><i><a href="/wiki/Shahnameh" title="Shahnameh">Shahnameh</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Ferdowsi" title="Ferdowsi">Ferdowsi</a> is the main collection of the mythology of Iran, which draws heavily on the stories and characters of <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>, from the texts of <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">Avesta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denkard" title="Denkard">Denkard</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bundahishn" title="Bundahishn">Bundahishn</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Observances">Observances</span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Iran" title="Public holidays in Iran">Public holidays in Iran</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Iranian_calendars" title="Iranian calendars">Iranian calendars</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:7SEEN_89.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/7SEEN_89.jpg/220px-7SEEN_89.jpg" width="220" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/7SEEN_89.jpg/330px-7SEEN_89.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/7SEEN_89.jpg/440px-7SEEN_89.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1199" data-file-height="1077"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:7SEEN_89.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Haft-Seen" title="Haft-Seen">Haft-Seen</a> (or 
        <i>Haft-Čin</i>), a customary of the 
        <a href="/wiki/Nowruz" title="Nowruz">Iranian New Year</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Iran has three official calendar systems, including the <a href="/wiki/Solar_Hijri_calendar" title="Solar Hijri calendar">Solar calendar</a> as the main, the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a> for international and Christian events, and the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Lunar calendar</a> for Islamic events.</p> 
     <p>The main national annual of Iran is <a href="/wiki/Nowruz" title="Nowruz">Nowruz</a>, an ancient tradition celebrated on 21 March to mark the beginning of spring and the <a href="/wiki/New_Year" title="New Year">New Year</a> of Iran. It is enjoyed by people with different religions, but is a holiday for Zoroastrians. It was registered on the list of <a href="/wiki/Masterpieces_of_the_Oral_and_Intangible_Heritage_of_Humanity" title="Masterpieces of the Oral and Intangible Heritage of Humanity">Masterpieces of the Oral and Intangible Heritage of Humanity</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Unesco.org_November_29_2015c_331-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Unesco.org_November_29_2015c-331">[330]</a></sup> and was described as the <i>Persian New Year</i><sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">[331]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">[332]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">[333]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">[334]</a></sup> by UNESCO in 2009.</p> 
     <p>Other remained national annuals of Iran include:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Chaharshanbe_Suri" title="Chaharshanbe Suri">Čā'r Šanbe Suri</a>: A prelude to Nowruz, in honor of <a href="/wiki/Atar" title="Atar">Ātar</a> (the <i>Holy Fire</i>), celebrated by fireworks and fire-jumping, on the last Wednesday before Nowruz</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Sizdah_Bedar" title="Sizdah Bedar" class="mw-redirect">Sizda' be Dar</a>: Leaving the house to join the nature, on the thirteenth day of the New Year (April 2)</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Yalda" title="Yalda" class="mw-redirect">Čelle ye Zemestān</a>: Also known as <i>Yaldā</i>; the longest night of the year, celebrated on the eve of <a href="/wiki/Winter_Solstice" title="Winter Solstice" class="mw-redirect">Winter Solstice</a>, by reciting poetry and having the customary fruits which include <a href="/wiki/Watermelon" title="Watermelon">watermelon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pomegranate" title="Pomegranate">pomegranate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mixed_nuts" title="Mixed nuts">mixed nuts</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Tirgan" title="Tirgan">Tirgān</a>: A mid summer festival, in honor of <a href="/wiki/Tishtrya" title="Tishtrya">Tishtrya</a>, celebrated on <a href="/wiki/Tir_(month)" title="Tir (month)">Tir</a> 13 (July 4), by splashing water, reciting poetry, and having traditional dishes such as šole-zard and spinach soup</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Mehrgan" title="Mehrgan" class="mw-redirect">Mehrgān</a>: An autumn festival, in honor of <a href="/wiki/Mithra" title="Mithra">Mithra</a>, celebrated on <a href="/wiki/Mehr_(month)" title="Mehr (month)">Mehr</a> 16 (October 8), by family gathering and setting a table of sweets, flowers, and a mirror</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Sepandarmazgan" title="Sepandarmazgan" class="mw-redirect">Sepand Ārmazgān</a>: Dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Amesha_Spenta" title="Amesha Spenta">Ameša Spenta</a> (the <i>Holy Devotion</i>); celebrated by giving presents to partners, on <a href="/wiki/Esfand" title="Esfand">Esfand</a> 15 (February 24)</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>Along with the national celebrations, annuals such as <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramezān</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eid_al-Fitr" title="Eid al-Fitr">Eid e Fetr</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Day_of_Ashura" title="Day of Ashura">Ruz e Āšurā</a> are marked by Muslims; <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Noel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lent" title="Lent">Čelle ye Ruze</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Eid e Pāk</a> are celebrated by Christians; and the festivals <a href="/wiki/Purim" title="Purim">Purim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Passover" title="Passover">Eid e Fatir</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tu_BiShvat" title="Tu BiShvat">Tu Bišvāt</a> are celebrated by Jewish people in Iran.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Iran" title="Music of Iran">Music of Iran</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Karna-Persian-Instrument-Persepolis-Museum.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Karna-Persian-Instrument-Persepolis-Museum.JPG/220px-Karna-Persian-Instrument-Persepolis-Museum.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Karna-Persian-Instrument-Persepolis-Museum.JPG/330px-Karna-Persian-Instrument-Persepolis-Museum.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Karna-Persian-Instrument-Persepolis-Museum.JPG/440px-Karna-Persian-Instrument-Persepolis-Museum.JPG 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Karna-Persian-Instrument-Persepolis-Museum.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Zurna" title="Zurna">Karna</a>, an ancient Iranian musical instrument from the 6th century BC
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Iran is the apparent birthplace of the earliest complex instruments, as evidenced by the archaeological records found in Western Iran, dating back to the 3rd millennium BC.<sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">[335]</a></sup> The Iranian use of both vertical and horizontal angular <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">harps</a> have been documented at the sites Madaktu and <a href="/wiki/Kul-e_Farah" title="Kul-e Farah">Kul-e Farah</a>, with the largest collection of <a href="/wiki/Elamite" title="Elamite" class="mw-redirect">Elamite</a> instruments documented at Kul-e Farah. Multiple depictions of horizontal harps were also sculpted in Assyrian palaces, dating back between <a href="/wiki/865_BC" title="865 BC" class="mw-redirect">865</a> and 650 BC.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Cyropaedia" title="Cyropaedia">Cyropaedia</a></i> refers to a great number of singing women at the court of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Iran" title="Achaemenid Iran" class="mw-redirect">Achaemenid Iran</a>. <a href="/wiki/Athenaeus_of_Naucratis" title="Athenaeus of Naucratis" class="mw-redirect">Athenaeus of Naucratis</a> states that, by the time of the last Achaemenid king, <a href="/wiki/Artashata" title="Artashata" class="mw-redirect">Artashata</a> (336–330 BC), Achaemenid singing girls were captured by the <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonian</a> general, <a href="/wiki/Parmenion" title="Parmenion">Parmenion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">[336]</a></sup> Under the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a>, a type of epic music was taught to youth, depicting the national epics and myths which were later represented in the <i><a href="/wiki/Shahnameh" title="Shahnameh">Shahnameh</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Ferdowsi" title="Ferdowsi">Ferdowsi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">[337]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>History of the <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_music" title="Sassanid music" class="mw-redirect">Sassanid music</a> is better documented than the earlier periods, and is specially more evident in the <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrian" title="Zoroastrian" class="mw-redirect">Zoroastrian</a> contexts.<sup id="cite_ref-EI-mhphi_339-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EI-mhphi-339">[338]</a></sup> By the time of <a href="/wiki/Khosrow_II" title="Khosrow II">Khosrow II</a>, the Sassanid royal court was the host of prominent musicians, namely Ramtin, <a href="/wiki/Bamshad" title="Bamshad">Bamshad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nakisa" title="Nakisa" class="mw-redirect">Nakisa</a>, Azad, <a href="/wiki/Sarkash" title="Sarkash">Sarkash</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Barbad" title="Barbad">Barbad</a>.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Mehmoonifinal2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Mehmoonifinal2.jpg/220px-Mehmoonifinal2.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Mehmoonifinal2.jpg/330px-Mehmoonifinal2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Mehmoonifinal2.jpg/440px-Mehmoonifinal2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1692"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Mehmoonifinal2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> A 
        <a href="/wiki/Safavid_art" title="Safavid art">Safavid painting</a> at 
        <a href="/wiki/Hasht_Behesht" title="Hasht Behesht">Hasht Behesht</a>, depicting a 7th-century Iranian banquet
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Some Iranian traditional musical instruments include <a href="/wiki/Saz_(musical_instrument)" title="Saz (musical instrument)" class="mw-redirect">saz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tar_(string_instrument)" title="Tar (string instrument)">Persian tar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tar_(Azerbaijani_instrument)" title="Tar (Azerbaijani instrument)">Azerbaijani tar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dotar" title="Dotar" class="mw-redirect">dotar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Setar" title="Setar">setar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kamanche" title="Kamanche" class="mw-redirect">kamanche</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">harp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbat_(lute)" title="Barbat (lute)">barbat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Santur" title="Santur">santur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanbur" title="Tanbur">tanbur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qanun_(instrument)" title="Qanun (instrument)">qanun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daf" title="Daf">dap</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tonbak" title="Tonbak">tompak</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ney" title="Ney">ney</a>.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Iran_national_orchestra.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3f/Iran_national_orchestra.jpg/220px-Iran_national_orchestra.jpg" width="220" height="86" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3f/Iran_national_orchestra.jpg/330px-Iran_national_orchestra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/3f/Iran_national_orchestra.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="157"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Iran_national_orchestra.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Iran%27s_National_Orchestra" title="Iran's National Orchestra">National Orchestra of Iran</a>, conducted by 
        <a href="/wiki/Rouhollah_Khaleghi" title="Rouhollah Khaleghi" class="mw-redirect">Khaleghi</a> in the 1940s
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The first national music society of the modern-day Iran was founded by <a href="/wiki/Rouhollah_Khaleghi" title="Rouhollah Khaleghi" class="mw-redirect">Rouhollah Khaleghi</a> in the 1940s, with the <i>School of National Music</i> established in 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">[339]</a></sup> Today, the main orchestra of Iran include the <a href="/wiki/Iran%27s_National_Orchestra" title="Iran's National Orchestra">National Orchestra</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Melal_Orchestra" title="Melal Orchestra">Nations Orchestra</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Tehran_Symphony_Orchestra" title="Tehran Symphony Orchestra">Symphony Orchestra of Tehran</a>.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Iranian_pop_music" title="Iranian pop music" class="mw-redirect">Iranian pop music</a> emerged by the <a href="/wiki/Qajar_Dynasty" title="Qajar Dynasty" class="mw-redirect">Qajar Era</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">[340]</a></sup> It was led to major developments in the 1950s, by the emergence of stars such as <a href="/wiki/Viguen" title="Viguen">Viguen</a>, who was referred to as the "king of Persian Pop and Jazz."<sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">[341]</a></sup> The 1970s is known as a "Golden Age" for Iranian pop music, where a revolution was formed in the music industry of Iran, using indigenous instruments and forms and adding <a href="/wiki/Electric_guitar" title="Electric guitar">electric guitar</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hayedeh" title="Hayedeh">Hayedeh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Faramarz_Aslani" title="Faramarz Aslani">Faramarz Aslani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Farhad_Mehrad" title="Farhad Mehrad">Farhad Mehrad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Googoosh" title="Googoosh">Googoosh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ebi" title="Ebi">Ebi</a> are among the leading artists of this period.</p> 
     <p>The emergence of genres such as <a href="/wiki/Iranian_rock" title="Iranian rock" class="mw-redirect">modern rock</a> in the 1970s and <a href="/wiki/Iranian_hip_hop" title="Iranian hip hop">hip hop</a> in the 1980s, which replaced the outdated musical styles among the youth, followed major movements and influences in the music of Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">[342]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">[343]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">[344]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">[345]</a></sup></p> 
     <div style="clear:both;"></div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theater">Theater</span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles: 
      <a href="/wiki/Persian_theater" title="Persian theater" class="mw-redirect">Persian theater</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Persian_dance" title="Persian dance">Persian dance</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:City_Theater,_Tehran,_Iran.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/City_Theater%2C_Tehran%2C_Iran.jpg/220px-City_Theater%2C_Tehran%2C_Iran.jpg" width="220" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/City_Theater%2C_Tehran%2C_Iran.jpg/330px-City_Theater%2C_Tehran%2C_Iran.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/City_Theater%2C_Tehran%2C_Iran.jpg/440px-City_Theater%2C_Tehran%2C_Iran.jpg 2x" data-file-width="809" data-file-height="709"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:City_Theater,_Tehran,_Iran.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/City_Theater_of_Tehran" title="City Theater of Tehran">City Theater of Tehran</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Theater background of Iran dates back to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">antiquity</a>. The earliest recorded representations of dancing figures within Iran were found in prehistoric sites such as Tepe Siyalk and Tepe Mūsīān.<sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">[346]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The oldest initiation of theater and phenomena of acting among the people of Iran can be traced in the epic ceremonial theaters, such as <i>Soug e Sivash</i> and <i>Mogh Koshi</i> (<i>Megakhouni</i>), and also dances and theater narrations of Iranian mythological tales reported by <a href="/wiki/Herodotos" title="Herodotos" class="mw-redirect">Herodotos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a>.</p> 
     <p>There are several theatrical genres which emerged before the advent of cinema in Iran, including <i>Xeyme Shab Bazi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Puppetry" title="Puppetry">Puppetry</a>), <i>Saye Bazi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Shadow_play" title="Shadow play">Shadow play</a>), <i>Ru-howzi</i> (Comical plays), and <i><a href="/wiki/Tazieh" title="Tazieh" class="mw-redirect">Tazieh</a></i> (Sorrow plays).</p> 
     <p>Before the <a href="/wiki/1979_Revolution" title="1979 Revolution" class="mw-redirect">1979 Revolution</a>, the Iranian national stage had become a famous performing scene for known international artists and troupes,<sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">[347]</a></sup> with the <a href="/wiki/Roudaki_Hall" title="Roudaki Hall">Roudaki Hall</a> of Tehran constructed to function as the national stage for <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">opera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ballet" title="Ballet">ballet</a>. Opened on October 26, 1967, the hall is home to the <a href="/wiki/Tehran_Symphony_Orchestra" title="Tehran Symphony Orchestra">Symphony Orchestra of Tehran</a>, the Opera Orchestra of Tehran, and the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_National_Ballet_Company" title="Iranian National Ballet Company">Iranian National Ballet Company</a>, and continues now with <i>Vahdat Hall</i> as its official name.</p> 
     <p>The opera <i><a href="/wiki/Rostam_and_Sohrab_(opera)" title="Rostam and Sohrab (opera)">Rostam o Sohrab</a></i>, based on the epic of <i><a href="/wiki/Rostam_and_Sohrab" title="Rostam and Sohrab">Rostam and Sohrab</a></i> from <i><a href="/wiki/Shahnameh" title="Shahnameh">Shahnameh</a></i>, is an example of opera performances in the modern-day Iran.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinema_and_animation">Cinema and animation</span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles: 
      <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Iran" title="Cinema of Iran">Cinema of Iran</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iranian_animation" title="History of Iranian animation">History of Iranian animation</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The earliest examples of visual representations in Iranian history are traced back to the <a href="/wiki/Bas-relief" title="Bas-relief" class="mw-redirect">bas-reliefs</a> of <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, c. 500 BC. Persepolis was the ritual center of the ancient kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Achaemenids" title="Achaemenids" class="mw-redirect">Achaemenids</a>, and the figures at Persepolis remain bound by the rules of grammar and syntax of visual language.<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">[348]</a></sup> The Iranian visual arts reached a pinnacle by the <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_Era" title="Sassanid Era" class="mw-redirect">Sassanid Era</a>. A bas-relief from this period in <a href="/wiki/Taq_Bostan" title="Taq Bostan">Taq Bostan</a> depicts a complex hunting scene. Similar works from the period have been found to articulate movements and actions in a highly sophisticated manner. It is even possible to see a progenitor of the cinema close-up in one of these works of art, which shows a wounded wild pig escaping from the hunting ground.<sup id="cite_ref-horschamp1_350-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-horschamp1-350">[349]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>By the early 20th century, the five-year-old modern industry of cinema came to Iran. The first Iranian filmmaker was <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Ebrahim_Khan_Akkas_Bashi" title="Mirza Ebrahim Khan Akkas Bashi">Mirza Ebrahim Khan (Akkas Bashi)</a>, the official photographer of <a href="/wiki/Mozaffar_ad-Din_Shah_Qajar" title="Mozaffar ad-Din Shah Qajar">Mozaffar od Din Shah</a> of <a href="/wiki/Qajar" title="Qajar" class="mw-redirect">Qajar</a>. He obtained a camera and filmed the Shah's visit to Europe.</p> 
     <p>In 1904, <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Ebrahim_Khan_Sahhafbashi" title="Mirza Ebrahim Khan Sahhafbashi">Mirza Ebrahim Khan (Sahhaf Bashi)</a> opened the first movie theater in Tehran.<sup id="cite_ref-massoudmehrabi1_351-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-massoudmehrabi1-351">[350]</a></sup> After him, several others like Russi Khan, Ardeshir Khan, and Ali Vakili tried to establish new movie theaters in Tehran. Until the early 1930s, there were around 15 cinema theaters in Tehran and 11 in other provinces.<sup id="cite_ref-horschamp1_350-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-horschamp1-350">[349]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The first silent Iranian film was made by Professor <a href="/wiki/Ovanes_Ohanian" title="Ovanes Ohanian">Ovanes Ohanian</a> in 1930, and the first sounded one, <i><a href="/wiki/Lor_Girl" title="Lor Girl">Lor Girl</a></i>, was made by <a href="/wiki/Abdolhossein_Sepanta" title="Abdolhossein Sepanta">Abd ol Hossein Sepanta</a> in 1932.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Behrouz_Vossoughi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Behrouz_Vossoughi.jpg/170px-Behrouz_Vossoughi.jpg" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Behrouz_Vossoughi.jpg/255px-Behrouz_Vossoughi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Behrouz_Vossoughi.jpg/340px-Behrouz_Vossoughi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="435" data-file-height="579"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Behrouz_Vossoughi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Behrouz_Vossoughi" title="Behrouz Vossoughi">Behrouz Vossoughi</a>, a well-known Iranian actor who has appeared in more than 90 films, with over 40 years of experience in motion picture industry
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The 1960s was a significant decade for Iranian cinema, with 25 commercial films produced annually on average throughout the early 60s, increasing to 65 by the end of the decade. The majority of production focused on melodrama and thrillers. With the screening of the films <i><a href="/wiki/Qeysar_(film)" title="Qeysar (film)">Kaiser</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cow_(film)" title="The Cow (film)">The Cow</a></i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Masoud_Kimiai" title="Masoud Kimiai">Masoud Kimiai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dariush_Mehrjui" title="Dariush Mehrjui">Dariush Mehrjui</a> respectively in 1969, alternative films established their status in the film industry. Attempts to organize a film festival that had begun in 1954 within the framework of the Golrizan Festival, bore fruits in the form of the Sepas Festival in 1969. The endeavors also resulted in the formation of the Tehran World Festival in 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-Esfandiary2012_352-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Esfandiary2012-352">[351]</a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Kiarostami-1940.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Kiarostami-1940.jpg/170px-Kiarostami-1940.jpg" width="170" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Kiarostami-1940.jpg/255px-Kiarostami-1940.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b6/Kiarostami-1940.jpg 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="310"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Kiarostami-1940.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Abbas_Kiarostami" title="Abbas Kiarostami">Abbas Kiarostami</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>After the Revolution of 1979, as the new government imposed new laws and standards, a new age in Iranian cinema emerged, starting with <i>Viva...</i> by <a href="/wiki/Khosrow_Sinai" title="Khosrow Sinai">Khosrow Sinai</a> and followed by many other directors, such as <a href="/wiki/Abbas_Kiarostami" title="Abbas Kiarostami">Abbas Kiarostami</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jafar_Panahi" title="Jafar Panahi">Jafar Panahi</a>. Kiarostami, an admired Iranian director, planted Iran firmly on the map of world cinema when he won the <a href="/wiki/Palme_d%27Or" title="Palme d'Or">Palme d'Or</a> for <i><a href="/wiki/Taste_of_Cherry" title="Taste of Cherry">Taste of Cherry</a></i> in 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-Dabashi2007_353-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dabashi2007-353">[352]</a></sup> The continuous presence of Iranian films in prestigious international festivals, such as the <a href="/wiki/Cannes_Film_Festival" title="Cannes Film Festival">Cannes Film Festival</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Venice_Film_Festival" title="Venice Film Festival">Venice Film Festival</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_International_Film_Festival" title="Berlin International Film Festival">Berlin International Film Festival</a>, attracted world attention to Iranian masterpieces.<sup id="cite_ref-DecherneyAtwood2014_354-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DecherneyAtwood2014-354">[353]</a></sup> In 2006, six Iranian films, of six different styles, represented Iranian cinema at the Berlin International Film Festival. Critics considered this a remarkable event in the history of Iranian cinema.<sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">[354]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">[355]</a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Asghar_Farhadi" title="Asghar Farhadi">Asghar Farhadi</a>, a well-known Iranian director, has received a <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Award" title="Golden Globe Award">Golden Globe Award</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award" class="mw-redirect">Academy Award</a> for <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Foreign_Language_Film" title="Academy Award for Best Foreign Language Film">Best Foreign Language Film</a>, and was named as one of the 100 Most Influential People in the world by <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time Magazine</a> in 2012.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Vase_animation.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Vase_animation.svg/220px-Vase_animation.svg.png" width="220" height="39" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Vase_animation.svg/330px-Vase_animation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Vase_animation.svg/440px-Vase_animation.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2384" data-file-height="426"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Vase_animation.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Reproduction of the world's oldest example of animation, dating back to the late half of the 3rd millennium BC, found in 
        <a href="/wiki/Burnt_City" title="Burnt City" class="mw-redirect">Burnt City</a>, Iran
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The oldest records of animation in Iran date back to the late 3rd millennium BC. An earthen goblet discovered at the site of the 5,200-year-old <a href="/wiki/Burnt_City" title="Burnt City" class="mw-redirect">Burnt City</a> in southeastern Iran, depicts what could possibly be the world’s oldest example of animation. The artifact bears five sequential images depicting a <a href="/wiki/Wild_goat" title="Wild goat">Persian ibex</a> jumping up to eat the leaves of a tree.<sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">[356]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">[357]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The art of animation, as practiced in modern Iran, started in the 1950s. After four decades of Iranian animation production and three-decade experience of <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_the_Intellectual_Development_of_Children_and_Young_Adults" title="Institute for the Intellectual Development of Children and Young Adults">Kanoon Institute</a>, the Tehran International Animation Festival (TIAF) was established in February 1999. Every two years, participants from more than 70 countries attend this event in Tehran, which holds Iran's biggest national animation market.<sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">[358]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-360" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-360">[359]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Iran" title="Sport in Iran">Sport in Iran</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Azadistadium.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Azadistadium.jpg/220px-Azadistadium.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Azadistadium.jpg/330px-Azadistadium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8f/Azadistadium.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="258"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Azadistadium.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Azadi_Stadium" title="Azadi Stadium">Azadi Stadium</a>, 
        <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">Tehran</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>With two thirds of Iran's population under the age of 25, many sports are played in Iran, both traditional and modern.</p> 
     <p>Iran is the birthplace of <a href="/wiki/Polo" title="Polo">polo</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">[360]</a></sup> known as <i>čowgān</i> in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Pahlavani" title="Pahlavani" class="mw-redirect">košti e pahlevāni</a></i>, meaning the "heroic <a href="/wiki/Wrestling" title="Wrestling">wrestling</a>." <a href="/wiki/Freestyle_wrestling" title="Freestyle wrestling">Freestyle wrestling</a> has been traditionally regarded as Iran's <a href="/wiki/National_sport" title="National sport">national sport</a>, where the national team <a href="/wiki/Iran_national_freestyle_wrestling_athletes" title="Iran national freestyle wrestling athletes">has been Olympic and world champion</a>.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Football_in_Iran" title="Football in Iran">Football</a> is known as the most popular sport in Iran, with <a href="/wiki/Iran_national_football_team" title="Iran national football team">the national team</a> having won the <a href="/wiki/Asian_Cup" title="Asian Cup" class="mw-redirect">Asian Cup</a> on three occasions.</p> 
     <p>Volleyball has been Iran's second most popular sport.<sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">[361]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-363" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-363">[362]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iran_men%27s_national_volleyball_team" title="Iran men's national volleyball team">Men's national team</a> ranked fourth in <a href="/wiki/2014_FIVB_Volleyball_World_League" title="2014 FIVB Volleyball World League">2014 FIVB Volleyball World League</a>, ranked sixth in <a href="/wiki/2014_FIVB_Volleyball_Men%27s_World_Championship" title="2014 FIVB Volleyball Men's World Championship">2014 FIVB Volleyball Men's World Championship</a>, and achieved the best result among the Asian national teams.<sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364">[363]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">[364]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-366" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-366">[365]</a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Dizin,_Iran.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Dizin%2C_Iran.jpeg/220px-Dizin%2C_Iran.jpeg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Dizin%2C_Iran.jpeg/330px-Dizin%2C_Iran.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Dizin%2C_Iran.jpeg/440px-Dizin%2C_Iran.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="789" data-file-height="526"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Dizin,_Iran.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Skiers at the 
        <a href="/wiki/Dizin" title="Dizin">Dizin Ski Resort</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Being a mountainous country, Iran is a venue for <a href="/wiki/Hiking" title="Hiking">hiking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rock_climbing" title="Rock climbing">rock climbing</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-367" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-367">[366]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mountain_climbing" title="Mountain climbing" class="mw-redirect">mountain climbing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-368" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-368">[367]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">[368]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Iran is home to many skiing resorts, the most famous being <a href="/wiki/Tochal" title="Tochal">Tochal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dizin" title="Dizin">Dizin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shemshak" title="Shemshak">Shemshak</a>, which are all within one to three hours traveling time from the city of <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">Tehran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Snowseasoncentral.com_November_29_2015c_370-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snowseasoncentral.com_November_29_2015c-370">[369]</a></sup> Tochal resort is the world's fifth-highest ski resort (3,730&nbsp;m or 12,238&nbsp;ft at its highest station).</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">Basketball</a> is also popular in Iran, where the <a href="/wiki/Iran_national_basketball_team" title="Iran national basketball team">national team</a> has won three <a href="/wiki/FIBA_Asia_Championship" title="FIBA Asia Championship">Asian Championships</a> since 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371">[370]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In <a href="/wiki/1974_Asian_Games" title="1974 Asian Games">1974</a>, Iran became the first country in West Asia to host the <a href="/wiki/Asian_Games" title="Asian Games">Asian Games</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Iranian_cuisine" title="Iranian cuisine">Iranian cuisine</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Kuku_Sabzi_(Iranian_food).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Kuku_Sabzi_%28Iranian_food%29.jpg/220px-Kuku_Sabzi_%28Iranian_food%29.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Kuku_Sabzi_%28Iranian_food%29.jpg/330px-Kuku_Sabzi_%28Iranian_food%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Kuku_Sabzi_%28Iranian_food%29.jpg/440px-Kuku_Sabzi_%28Iranian_food%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="347"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Kuku_Sabzi_(Iranian_food).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <i><a href="/wiki/Kuku_(food)" title="Kuku (food)">Kuku</a> Sabzi</i> with herbs, topped with 
        <a href="/wiki/Barberries" title="Barberries" class="mw-redirect">barberries</a> and 
        <a href="/wiki/Walnuts" title="Walnuts" class="mw-redirect">walnuts</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Iranian cuisine is diverse due to its variety of ethnic groups and the influence of other cultures. Herbs are frequently used along with fruits such as plums, pomegranates, quince, prunes, apricots, and raisins. Iranians usually eat plain <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a> with lunch and dinner; it is a staple of the diet in Iran. To achieve a balanced taste, characteristic flavourings such as saffron, dried limes, cinnamon, and parsley are mixed delicately and used in some special dishes. Onions and garlic are normally used in the preparation of the accompanying course, but are also served separately during meals, either in raw or pickled form. Iran is also famous for its <a href="/wiki/Caviar" title="Caviar">caviar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-372" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-372">[371]</a></sup></p>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2> 
     <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;"> 
      <ol class="references"> 
       <li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the Avesta the <i>airiia-</i> are members of the ethnic group of the Avesta-reciters themselves, in contradistinction to the <i>anairiia-</i>, the "<a href="/wiki/Aniran" title="Aniran">non-Aryas</a>". The word also appears four times in Old Persian: One is in the <a href="/wiki/Behistun_inscription" title="Behistun inscription" class="mw-redirect">Behistun inscription</a>, where <i>ariya-</i> is the name of a language or script (DB 4.89). The other three instances occur in <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius I</a>'s inscription at <a href="/wiki/Naqsh-e_Rustam" title="Naqsh-e Rustam">Naqsh-e Rustam</a> (DNa 14-15), in Darius I's inscription at Susa (DSe 13-14), and in the inscription of <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a> at <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a> (XPh 12-13). In these, the two Achaemenid dynasts describe themselves as <i>pārsa pārsahyā puça ariya ariyaciça</i> "a <a href="/wiki/Fars_province" title="Fars province" class="mw-redirect">Persian</a>, son of a Persian, an Ariya, of Ariya origin." "The phrase with <i>ciça</i>, “origin, descendance,” assures that it [i.e. <i>ariya</i>] is an ethnic name wider in meaning than <i>pārsa</i> and not a simple adjectival epithet."<sup id="cite_ref-Bailey_Arya_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey_Arya-40">[40]</a></sup></span></li> 
      </ol> 
     </div>

       <li id="cite_note-AlirezaShahbazi-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-AlirezaShahbazi_5-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Alireza Shapur Shahbazi (2005), "The History of the Idea of Iran", in Vesta Curtis ed., Birth of the Persian Empire, IB Tauris, London, p. 108: "Similarly the collapse of Sassanian Eranshahr in AD 650 did not end Iranians' national idea. The name 'Iran' disappeared from official records of the Saffarids, Samanids, Buyids, Saljuqs and their successor. But one unofficially used the name Iran, Eranshahr, and similar national designations, particularly Mamalek-e Iran or "Iranian lands", which exactly translated the old Avestan term Ariyanam Daihunam. On the other hand, when the Safavids (not Reza Shah, as is popularly assumed) revived a national state officially known as Iran, bureaucratic usage in the Ottoman empire and even Iran itself could still refer to it by other descriptive and traditional appellations".</span></li>

       <li id="cite_note-1996.2C_p._47-71"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1996.2C_p._47_71-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Yarshater (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=WBAZAQAAIAAJ&amp;q=%28Meyer+p.85%29">1996, p. 47</a>)</span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-Cook-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Cook_72-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">While estimates for the Achaemenid Empire range from 10–80+ million, most prefer 50 million. Prevas (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=1S-Q-6jDZ7AC&amp;pg=PA14">2009, p. 14</a>) estimates 10 million. Strauss (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=nQFtMcD5dOsC&amp;pg=PA37">2004, p. 37</a>) estimates about 20 million. Ward (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=8eUTLaaVOOQC&amp;pg=PA16">2009, p. 16</a>) estimates at 20 million. Scheidel (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=6vnkts2rOJUC&amp;pg=PA99">2009, p. 99</a>) estimates 35 million. Daniel (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=AzqbYf9Q_2UC&amp;pg=PA41">2001, p. 41</a>) estimates at 50 million. Meyer and Andreades (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Uwn9d01zMvAC&amp;pg=PA58">2004, p. 58</a>) estimates to 50 million. Jones (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=75ueY2rqTYMC&amp;pg=PA8">2004, p. 8</a>) estimates over 50 million. Richard (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=toJI-Z_WAC0C&amp;pg=PA34">2008, p. 34</a>) estimates nearly 70 million. Hanson (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=6p5mAAAAMAAJ">2001, p. 32</a>) estimates almost 75 million. Cowley (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=l6JmAAAAMAAJ">1999 and 2001, p. 17</a>) estimates possibly 80 million.</span></li>

       <li id="cite_note-290"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-290">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Rasmus Christian Elling, Minorities in Iran: Nationalism and Ethnicity after Khomeini, Palgrave Macmillan, 2013. Excerpt: "The number of Azeris in Iran is heavily disputed. In 2005, Amanolahi estimated all Turkic-speaking communities in Iran to number no more than 9 million. CIA and Library of congress estimates range from 16 percent to 24 percent – that is, 12–18 million people if we employ the latest total figure for Iran's population (77.8 million). Azeri ethnicsts, on the other hand, argue that overall number is much higher, even as much as 50 percent or more of the total population. Such inflated estimates may have influenced some Western scholars who suggest that up to 30 percent (that is, some 23 million today) Iranians are Azeris." <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=rWEbrv5oD8AC&amp;pg=PT33&amp;dq=argue+that+overall+number+is+much+higher,+even+as+much+as+50+percent+azeri&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=WVJrUcnJE62g4AO5n4HADw&amp;ved=0CC4Q6AEwAA">[1]</a></span></li>
